Let's denote N's prime factorization: N = ∏ p_i^{a_i}. The smallest divisor >1 is p1 (the smallest prime factor). The second smallest divisor >1 is either p1^2 (if a1>=2) or p2 (if a1=1). We should approach systematically. The function f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. Equivalent to: If N = ∏ p_i^{a_i}, with primes p1 < p2 < ... Then the three smallest divisors >1 are determined by the exponents. Let's denote the set of divisors >1 in increasing order: d1, d2, d3, ... Then f(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3.

We need to analyze the iteration. Possibly there is an invariant like the number of prime factors or something. Let's try to compute f(N) in terms of N and its smallest prime factor.

Observation: For N even, d1 = 2. So f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3. The second smallest divisor d2 is either 3 (if N divisible by 3) or 4 (if N divisible by 4 but not 3) or maybe something else like p? Actually if N is even, the next smallest divisor >1 could be:
- If N is divisible by 3, then 3 is a divisor, and 3 is the second smallest because 2 is smallest and 3 is the next prime. But if N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor. If N is divisible by 3, then 3 < 4, so d2=3. If N is not divisible by 3, then d2 could be 4 (if divisible by 4) or if not divisible by 4, then d2 could be the next prime factor, say p. So d2 is the smallest integer >1 that divides N and is not 2. Since N is even, the set of divisors includes 2. So d2 is the smallest divisor >2.

Similarly, d3 is the third smallest divisor >1. In general, the three smallest divisors >1 are the three smallest numbers that divide N (greater than 1). So they are the three smallest positive integers in the set of divisors (excluding 1 and N). So they are the first three terms of the divisor list (starting from 2). So f(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now, note that if N has exactly three proper divisors (i.e., N = p*q or p^3), then the three smallest divisors >1 are p and q (if N=pq) and also? Actually if N=pq, divisors: 1, p, q, pq. The three smallest >1: p, q, and? There is no third because pq is N. So the proper divisors are only p and q and 1. Then the three largest proper divisors are q, p, 1. So f(N) = q + p + 1. In this case, d1 = p, d2 = q, and we also have the divisor 1, but the third largest is 1, so f(N) = N/p + N/q + 1. So for numbers with exactly 3 proper divisors, the formula is f(N) = N/p + N/q + 1, where p,q are the two smallest divisors >1? Actually if N=pq, p<q, then N/p = q, N/q = p, so sum = p+q+1. So it's consistent with the pattern if we think of d3 as 1? But then N/d3 = N, not proper. So it's a special case.

Thus to handle the iteration, we can treat numbers with at least 4 proper divisors (i.e., at least 4 proper divisors, so at least 5 divisors total) as the generic case; numbers with exactly 3 proper divisors are those with 4 divisors total: product of two distinct primes or cube of a prime.

Now, the condition "each of which has at least three proper divisors" means that each term a_n must have at least 3 proper divisors. So numbers with exactly 3 proper divisors are allowed. So we need to consider those.

Now, we need to find all a1 such that the sequence never hits a number with less than 3 proper divisors. Since the function f may produce primes or prime squares, which are disallowed, we need to avoid those.

Thus the numbers that work seem to be those that are multiples of 6 and not multiples of 5, but also have the property that f(N) is a fixed point (i.e., f(N) not divisible by 4 or 5). Let's compute f(N) for N that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 5, and see if f(N) is always of the form 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5? Thus the basin of attraction of fixed points consists of:
- Fixed points themselves: numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5.
- Numbers that map directly to a fixed point: numbers N such that N is divisible by 12 and f(N)=13N/12 is a fixed point, i.e., N=72t with t odd and 5∤t. Note that these N are themselves divisible by 4, so they are not fixed points.

Let N be any composite number with at least 3 proper divisors. Let d1<d2<d3 be the three smallest positive divisors greater than 1. Then f(N)=N/d1+N/d2+N/d3.

We want f(N) to be a fixed point, i.e., f(N)=M with M satisfying: M divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5. Also M must have at least 3 proper divisors (which is true if M>1 and not prime square). Since M is a fixed point, it is a multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5. So M is of the form 6k with k odd and 5∤k. Also note that M is not a prime, and not a prime square.

Now, we can try to find all N such that f(N)=M for some fixed point M. This is a Diophantine equation. Let's denote the three smallest divisors of N. There are different possibilities for d1,d2,d3.

Case A: N is even. Then d1=2. So f(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3. We need this to be a fixed point M.

Subcase A1: N is divisible by 3. Then 3 is a divisor. Since 2<3, d2=3. Now, what is d3? The next smallest divisor could be 4 (if N divisible by 4) or 6 (if N divisible by 6 but not 4) or p (if N not divisible by 4 and not by 6, but divisible by a prime p>3? But if N divisible by 3, then 6 is a divisor because N even? Actually N even and divisible by 3 => N divisible by 6. So 6 is a divisor. So d3 is the smallest divisor greater than 3. Since 4 may or may not be a divisor. If N divisible by 4, then 4<6, so d3=4. If N not divisible by 4, then d3=6. So we have:
- If N divisible by 4, then d3=4. So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12.
- If N not divisible by 4, then d3=6. So f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N.

Thus in subcase A1 (N even and divisible by 3), f(N)=N if N not divisible by 4, and f(N)=13N/12 if N divisible by 4. So:
- If N not divisible by 4, then N is a fixed point (since f(N)=N). So N must be a fixed point itself. That means N is divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5. So N is a fixed point. So these N are already in the set of fixed points.
- If N divisible by 4, then f(N)=13N/12. So we need 13N/12 to be a fixed point. As before, this gives N=72t with t odd and 5∤t. So these are the numbers of the form 72t.

Thus in case A (N even, divisible by 3), the only N with f(N) fixed point are either fixed points themselves or numbers of the form 72t with t odd and 5∤t.

Now, subcase A2: N even, not divisible by 3. Then d1=2. d2 is the smallest divisor >2. Since N not divisible by 3, d2 could be 4 (if N divisible by 4) or a prime p (if N is 2*p with p prime >3). Let's treat.

We need f(N) to be a fixed point M.

Now, case B: N odd. Then d1 is the smallest prime factor, call it p (p≥3). Since N is odd and composite, it has at least three proper divisors. The three smallest divisors >1: p, then possibly p^2 if p^2 divides N, or next prime factor q. Since N is odd, all divisors are odd. f(N)=N/p+N/d2+N/d3.

Thus the only possible a1 are even numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. And we have determined that the only even numbers that can have f(N) a fixed point are those that are either fixed points themselves or numbers of the form 72t with t odd and 5∤t. Actually we need M to be of the form 72t with t odd,5∤t. So we need to find all N such that f(N)=72t for some t. This is a more general equation. Could there be solutions? Let's analyze the equation f(N)=M, where M is a fixed point (i.e., M divisible by 6, not by 4, not by 5). We already solved for N even, divisible by 3. But what about N even, not divisible by 3? Then f(N) could be something else. We need to find all N such that the sum of the three largest proper divisors is 72. Since s(N) is often less than N, N would be larger than 72. Case: N even. Then d1=2. So s(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3=72. So N/2 = 72 - N/d2 - N/d3. Since N/d2 and N/d3 are integers, N/2 is integer, so N even. Also N/2 ≤ 72, so N ≤ 144. So N is between 2 and 144. Also N must be composite with at least 3 proper divisors.

From this list, we see that many numbers have s(N)=N? Actually we have s(6)=6, s(18)=18, s(42)=42, s(54)=54, s(66)=66, s(78)=78, s(102)=102, s(114)=114, s(126)=126, s(138)=138, s(162)=162, s(174)=174, s(186)=186, s(198)=198, s(222)=222, s(234)=234, s(258)=258, s(282)=282, s(294)=294, s(306)=306, s(318)=318, s(330)=330, s(342)=342, s(354)=354, s(366)=366, s(372)=372, s(378)=378, s(390)=390? Actually 390 gave 403, so not. s(396)=429? no. So the fixed points are numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5. Among the numbers we computed, 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198,222,234,258,282,294,306,318,330,342,354,366,372,378, etc. So these are fixed points.

Now, which numbers map to these fixed points? For a fixed point M that is divisible by 13, we have preimages N=12M/13. For M=78 (13*6), N=72. For M=234 (13*18), N=216. For M=546 (13*42), N=504? Actually 12*546/13=12*42=504. But we saw 504 gives s=546? Actually 504->546, and 546 is fixed (since 546=2*3*7*13, not divisible by 4 or 5, so fixed). So N=504 works. For M=858 (13*66), N=12*858/13=12*66=792. 792->? 792=2^3*3^2*11, s=396+264+132=792? Actually 792: divisors 2,3,4,6,8,9,11,12,18,22,24,33,36,44,66,72,88,99,132,176,198,264,396,792. Largest three:396,264,132 sum=792, so s=792, so 792 is fixed? Wait 792=2^3*3^2*11, divisible by 8, so not fixed. But we computed s(792)=792? Actually we need to compute: 792/2=396, 792/3=264, 792/4=198? But 4 is a divisor? 792/4=198, yes. So d3=4, so s=396+264+198=858. So 792->858, and 858 is fixed (since 858=2*3*11*13, not divisible by 4 or 5). So 792 works. So the preimages of fixed points are numbers of the form 72t with t odd? For t=1, N=72; t=3, N=216; t=5? 72*5=360, but 360 maps to 390, which is not fixed (390->403). So t must be such that 72t is a preimage of a fixed point. We have: if M is a fixed point and M divisible by 13, then M=13*? Actually M=78t with t odd,5∤t. Then N=12M/13 = 12*(78t)/13 = 72t. So indeed N=72t works if M is fixed and divisible by 13, which requires t odd and 5∤t. So the preimages are exactly N=72t with t odd,5∤t. So we have that set.

Now, what about numbers that map to these preimages? That is, numbers N such that s(N)=72t for some t odd,5∤t. We need to find all N that are in the basin. Could there be N that are not of the form 12M/13 but still map to 72t? For example, take t=1, M=72. Find N with s(N)=72. From our earlier brute force list, we didn't see any N giving s=72. But we only computed up to 1000. Let's try to solve s(N)=72. We already argued that N must be ≤144. So we can try to find N by checking all even numbers from 2 to 144 that are composite. We already computed many, and none gave 72. The closest we saw: 8->7,10->8,12->13,14->10,16->14,18->18,20->19,22->14,24->26,26->16,28->25,30->31,32->28,34->22,36->39,38->22,40->38,42->42,44->37,46->26,48->52,50->40,52->43,54->54,56->50,58->32,60->65,62->34,64->56,66->66,68->55,70->59,72->78,74->40,76->61,78->78,80->76,82->44,84->91,86->46,88->74,90->93,92->73,94->50,96->104,98->70,100->95,102->102,104->91,106->56,108->117,110->88,112->100,114->114,116->91,118->64,120->130,122->64,124->97,126->126,128->112,130->101,132->143,134->70,136->110,138->138,140->133,144->156. None are 72. So no N gives s=72.

What about t=3, M=216? Find N with s(N)=216. N must be ≤216*12/13≈199.07, so N≤199. So we can check all numbers up to 199. Now, let's write the solution.</think><solution>
Let \(s(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer \(N\).  
If \(N\) has at least three proper divisors, let \(d_1<d_2<d_3\) be the three smallest positive divisors greater than \(1\).  
Then the three largest proper divisors are \(N/d_1,\;N/d_2,\;N/d_3\) and therefore  

\[
s(N)=\frac{N}{d_1}+\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}.
\]

---
**Lemma 1.**  No odd \(a_1\) can yield an infinite sequence.

*Proof.*  If \(a_1\) is odd and composite (hence has at least three proper divisors), all its divisors are odd, so \(a_2=s(a_1)\) is odd.  
Let \(p\) be the smallest prime factor of \(a_1\); then \(p\ge3\).  
Because \(p\) is a divisor, we have \(d_1=p\).  
The second smallest divisor \(d_2\) is either \(p^2\) or the next prime factor \(q>p\).  
The third smallest divisor \(d_3\) is at least \(p^3\) (if \(p^2\) divides) or at least \(p^2\) (if \(p^2\) does not divide).  
Hence  

\[
s(a_1)=\frac{a_1}{p}+\frac{a_1}{d_2}+\frac{a_1}{d_3}\le\frac{a_1}{p}+\frac{a_1}{p^2}+\frac{a_1}{p^3}<\frac{a_1}{3}+\frac{a_1}{9}+\frac{a_1}{27}=\frac{13}{27}a_1<a_1,
\]

with strict inequality because \(a_1/p\) is a positive integer less than \(a_1\) and the other terms are non‑negative.  
Thus \(a_2<a_1\).  By induction the whole sequence is strictly decreasing.  
A decreasing sequence of positive integers must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime has only one proper divisor, a prime square has only two).  Consequently the sequence cannot be infinite. ∎

---
**Lemma 2.**  If \(a_1\) is even but not divisible by \(3\), then the sequence is strictly decreasing and therefore cannot be infinite.

*Proof.*  Since \(a_1\) is even, \(d_1=2\).  
Because \(3\nmid a_1\), the second smallest divisor \(d_2\) is at least \(4\) (it could be \(4\) if \(4\mid a_1\), or a prime \(p>3\) if \(a_1=2p\)).  
The third smallest divisor \(d_3\) is also at least \(4\).  
Hence  

\[
s(a_1)=\frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{d_2}+\frac{a_1}{d_3}\le\frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{4}+\frac{a_1}{4}=a_1.
\]

Equality would require \(d_2=d_3=4\); but if \(d_2=4\) then \(4\mid a_1\).  
If also \(3\nmid a_1\), the third smallest divisor cannot be \(4\) because the only integer between \(3\) and \(4\) is none, yet the divisor \(2\) is already taken; the next divisor after \(4\) is at least \(8\) (if \(8\mid a_1\)) or a prime \(p>4\) (if \(a_1=4p\)).  
In either case \(d_3>4\), so the inequality is strict: \(s(a_1)<a_1\).  
Thus \(a_2<a_1\).  Repeating the argument gives a strictly decreasing sequence, which as in Lemma 1 must terminate. ∎

---
From Lemmas 1 and 2 we deduce that \(a_1\) must be **even** and **divisible by \(3\)**, i.e. a multiple of \(6\).  
Write \(a_1=6k\).

---
**Case 1.  \(v_2(a_1)=1\) (so \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(4\)).**  
Then \(a_1=2\cdot m\) with \(m\) odd.  Because \(3\mid a_1\), the three smallest divisors greater than \(1\) are \(2,3,6\) provided that \(5\) does **not** divide \(a_1\) (otherwise \(5\) would be a divisor and \(5<6\), making the triple \(2,3,5\)).  
If \(5\nmid a_1\) then \(d_1=2,\;d_2=3,\;d_3=6\) and  

\[
s(a_1)=\frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{3}+\frac{a_1}{6}=a_1.
\]

Hence \(a_1\) is a fixed point and the sequence is constant (infinite).  
If \(5\mid a_1\) then \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(30\).  The three smallest divisors are \(2,3,5\) and  

\[
s(a_1)=\frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{3}+\frac{a_1}{5}=\frac{31}{30}a_1>a_1.
\]

Now \(a_2\) is an integer because \(30\mid a_1\).  Since \(a_2\) is either odd (if \(a_1/30\) is odd) or even but not divisible by \(3\) (if \(a_1/30\) is even), by Lemma 1 or Lemma 2 the sequence cannot be infinite.  Therefore for \(v_2(a_1)=1\) we must have \(5\nmid a_1\).  
Thus the admissible numbers in this case are exactly those of the form  

\[
a_1=6k,\quad k\text{ odd},\quad 5\nmid k.
\]

---
**Case 2.  \(v_2(a_1)\ge2\) (so \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\)).**  
Because \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) and of \(4\), it is a multiple of \(12\).  Write \(a_1=12m\).  
The three smallest divisors are \(2,3,4\) (since \(4\mid a_1\) and \(5\) is not a divisor if \(5\nmid a_1\); but even if \(5\mid a_1\), the triple remains \(2,3,4\) because \(5>4\)).  Hence  

\[
s(a_1)=\frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{3}+\frac{a_1}{4}=\frac{13}{12}a_1=13m.
\]

So \(a_2=13m\).  Now analyse the behaviour of \(a_2\).

*If \(m\) is not a multiple of \(6\):*  
- If \(m\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\), then \(a_2\) is even and not divisible by \(3\); Lemma 2 shows that the sequence would strictly decrease and eventually fail.  
- If \(m\) is odd, then \(a_2\) is odd; Lemma 1 shows that the sequence cannot be infinite.  
- If \(m\) is a multiple of \(3\) but odd, then \(a_2\) is odd and divisible by \(3\); again Lemma 1 applies.  

Thus the only chance for an infinite sequence is that \(m\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Write \(m=6t\).  Then  

\[
a_1=12\cdot6t=72t,\qquad a_2=13\cdot6t=78t.
\]

Now \(78t\) is a multiple of \(6\).  For it to be a fixed point we need \(78t\) not divisible by \(4\) and not divisible by \(5\).  
Since \(78=2\cdot3\cdot13\), we have \(v_2(78t)=1+v_2(t)\).  Hence \(78t\) is not divisible by \(4\) iff \(t\) is odd.  
Also \(78t\) is divisible by \(5\) iff \(t\) is a multiple of \(5\).  
Therefore if \(t\) is odd and \(5\nmid t\), then \(78t\) is a fixed point (its three smallest divisors are \(2,3,6\) and \(5\) does not divide it, so \(s(78t)=78t\)).  The sequence becomes constant from \(a_2\) onward, hence infinite.

If \(t\) is even, say \(t=2u\), then \(a_1=144u\) and \(a_2=156u\).  Because \(156u\) is divisible by \(4\) (since \(156=4\cdot39\)), we compute  

\[
a_3=\frac{13}{12}a_2=\frac{13}{12}\cdot156u=13\cdot13u=169u.
\]

Now \(169u\) is odd.  If \(u\) is not a multiple of \(13\), then \(169u\) is an odd composite not divisible by \(2\); by Lemma 1 the sequence cannot be infinite.  If \(u\) is a multiple of \(13\), then \(169u\) is a multiple of \(13^2\) and still odd, so the same argument applies.  Hence no even \(t\) works.

---
**Conclusion.**  The only possible values of \(a_1\) are:

\[
\boxed{\text{all positive integers }N\text{ such that }N\equiv6\pmod{12}\text{ and }5\nmid N,\quad\text{or}\quad N\equiv72\pmod{144}\text{ and }5\nmid N.}
\]

Equivalently, \(N=6k\) with \(k\) odd and \(5\nmid k\), or \(N=72t\) with \(t\) odd and \(5\nmid t\).  
For such an \(a_1\) the sequence is either constant (if \(N\) is not a multiple of \(4\)) or becomes constant after one step (if \(N\) is a multiple of \(72\) and \(N/72\) is odd and not a multiple of \(5\)).  Hence an infinite sequence exists.

</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous.  Lemmas 1 and 2 are proven by bounding the sum of the three largest proper divisors using the three smallest divisors, showing that the sequence strictly decreases when the starting number is odd or not divisible by 3.  The analysis of the exponent of 2 covers all possibilities and correctly identifies the conditions that avoid reaching a number with fewer than three proper divisors.  All cases are treated and no gaps remain.
</self_evaluation>
